<li id="cite_note-23"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-23">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Don S. Armentrout (1 January 2009). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=g4_P098HhHMC&amp;pg=PT16&amp;dq=St+Alban+first+Anglican+year+martyred&amp;hl=en&amp;sa=X&amp;ei=29UnU7rGGemkyQHx_oGwBA&amp;ved=0CDAQ6AEwAQ#v=onepage&amp;q=St%20Alban%20first%20Anglican%20year%20martyred&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow"><i>An Episcopal Dictionary of the Church</i></a>. Church Publishing Incorporated. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0898697018" title="Special:BookSources/0898697018">0898697018</a>. <q>Alban, St. (d.c. 304). First Christian martyr of Britain. The little known about him is from the Venerable Bede. Bede's story places Alban's martydom during the persecution of Diocletius (c. 304), but some scholars suggest that it may have occurred during the persecution of Decius (c. 254) or of Septimius Severus (c. 209).</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAnglicanism&amp;rft.au=Don+S.+Armentrout&amp;rft.btitle=An+Episcopal+Dictionary+of+the+Church&amp;rft.date=2009-01-01&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3Dg4_P098HhHMC%26pg%3DPT16%26dq%3DSt%2BAlban%2Bfirst%2BAnglican%2Byear%2Bmartyred%26hl%3Den%26sa%3DX%26ei%3D29UnU7rGGemkyQHx_oGwBA%26ved%3D0CDAQ6AEwAQ%23v%3Donepage%26q%3DSt%2520Alban%2520first%2520Anglican%2520year%2520martyred%26f%3Dfalse&amp;rft.isbn=0898697018&amp;rft.pub=Church+Publishing+Incorporated&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Heinrich_Zimmer.2C_Professor_of_Celtic_Philology_in_the_University_of_Berlin_107.E2.80.93109-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Heinrich_Zimmer.2C_Professor_of_Celtic_Philology_in_the_University_of_Berlin_107.E2.80.93109_24-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Heinrich_Zimmer.2C_Professor_of_Celtic_Philology_in_the_University_of_Berlin_107.E2.80.93109_24-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Heinrich Zimmer, Professor of Celtic Philology in the University of Berlin. <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=TlswAQAAMAAJ&amp;printsec=frontcover#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow"><i>The Celtic Church in Britain and Ireland</i></a>. Ballantyne, Hanson &amp; Co. pp. 107–109. <q>For although we differ widely from the current views with regard to the introduction and development of Irish Christianity down to the days of Columba, yet this does not affect the fundamental view, shared by most modern investigators, as to the relation of the institutions of the Celtic Church towards those of the Roman Church at the beginning of the seventh century. On the contrary, with regard to the Irish branch, this view receives fresh support from our statements. Neither from what tradition tells us about the doctrines and institutions of the Celtic Church, nor from what we know or may fairly conjecture about her history, do we receive any support for the hypothesis that the Celtic Church during her golden age greatly resembled the Church of the apostolic era in institutions and dogma. Just as Britain was a part of the Roman Empire, so the British Church formed (during the fourth century) a branch of the Catholic Church of the West; and during the whole of that century, from the council of Arles (316) onward, took part in all proceedings concerning the Church. But the Irish branch of the Celtic Church was an offshoot of that British Church, and had sprung up as early as the fourth century. At the beginning of the seventh century the institutions of the Celtic Church on either side of the Irish Sea showed divergences from the Church of Rome which are well attested. These, on a closer view, admit of full explanation. Above all, we must not forget the fact that in the Roman Catholic Church the position of the Roman bishop during the fourth century and up to the time of Leo the Great (440–461) differed from that of Pope Gregory the Great (509–604) at the end of the sixth century. At the beginning of the seventh century rigid uniformity of institutions was regarded as an essential requirement of the <i>unitas catholica ;</i> but to the fourth century this idea was wholly foreign. Besides, many innovations took long to domesticate themselves with the distant branches of the Church. At the end of the fourth century the British branch of the Catholic Church, together with its offshoot in the barbarian isle, were severed from Rome, because political Rome had lost its hold on Britain.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAnglicanism&amp;rft.au=Heinrich+Zimmer%2C+Professor+of+Celtic+Philology+in+the+University+of+Berlin&amp;rft.btitle=The+Celtic+Church+in+Britain+and+Ireland&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DTlswAQAAMAAJ%26printsec%3Dfrontcover%23v%3Donepage%26q%26f%3Dfalse&amp;rft.pages=107-109&amp;rft.pub=Ballantyne%2C+Hanson+%26+Co.&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-27"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-27">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Hogue, Richard. <i>A Theological History of Christian Glossolalia</i>. <a href="/wiki/Tate_Publishing_Ltd" title="Tate Publishing Ltd">Tate Publishing Ltd</a>. p. 160. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/1615666745" title="Special:BookSources/1615666745">1615666745</a>. <q>In AD 407, Rome withdrew its armies from Britain to defend Italy from Visigothic attack. With the sack of Rome in AD 410, legions of Rome never returned to Britain. The absence of Roman military and governmental influence and overall decline of Roman imperial political power enabled Britain and the surrounding isles to develop distinctively from the rest of the West. A new culture emerged around the Irish Sea among the Celtic peoples with Celtic Christianity at its core. What resulted was a form of Christianity distinct from Rome in many traditions and practices.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAnglicanism&amp;rft.aufirst=Richard&amp;rft.aulast=Hogue&amp;rft.btitle=A+Theological+History+of+Christian+Glossolalia&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=1615666745&amp;rft.pages=160&amp;rft.pub=Tate+Publishing+Ltd&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-28"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-28">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Irving Hexham; Stephen Rost; John Morehead. <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=XKK_mjQO5r0C&amp;pg=PA47&amp;dq=celtic+church+rejected+Rome+isolation&amp;hl=en&amp;sa=X&amp;ei=aYomU8bSEpCFyQGZ0IDYCQ&amp;ved=0CC8Q6AEwAQ#v=onepage&amp;q=celtic%20church%20rejected%20Rome%20isolation&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow"><i>Encountering New Religious Movements</i></a>. Kregel Academic. p. 48. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0825428939" title="Special:BookSources/0825428939">0825428939</a>. <q>Rooted in those years of Celtic Christian culture's isolation is its uniqueness, its mystery...Nowhere in this history of Christianity is there so clear an instance of the Christian transformation of a pagan culture with so little influence by the culture that brought the Christian message. For as soon as the Roman culture had carried the gospel to Ireland, the carrier collapsed....For several generations there was little influence from the rest of European Christianity and the result was a unique Christian blossoming of a formerly pagan culture.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAnglicanism&amp;rft.au=Irving+Hexham&amp;rft.au=John+Morehead&amp;rft.au=Stephen+Rost&amp;rft.btitle=Encountering+New+Religious+Movements&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DXKK_mjQO5r0C%26pg%3DPA47%26dq%3Dceltic%2Bchurch%2Brejected%2BRome%2Bisolation%26hl%3Den%26sa%3DX%26ei%3DaYomU8bSEpCFyQGZ0IDYCQ%26ved%3D0CC8Q6AEwAQ%23v%3Donepage%26q%3Dceltic%2520church%2520rejected%2520Rome%2520isolation%26f%3Dfalse&amp;rft.isbn=0825428939&amp;rft.pages=48&amp;rft.pub=Kregel+Academic&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Global-29"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Global_29-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Andrew Wingate; Kevin Ward; Carrie Pemberton; Wilson Sitshebo (1998). <i>Anglicanism: A Global Communion</i>. Church Publishing Incorporated. p. 52. <q>The essential starting point for any appreciation of Celtic Christianity must begin with its geographical origins in Scotland, Ireland and the Isle of Man, and Wales, Cornwall and Brittany, of two linguistically inter-related groups in countries which lay on the fringes of the Roman Empire. Thus for purely historical and geographical reasons they escaped the social, economic and cultural influence of Rome.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAnglicanism&amp;rft.au=Andrew+Wingate&amp;rft.au=Carrie+Pemberton&amp;rft.au=Kevin+Ward&amp;rft.au=Wilson+Sitshebo&amp;rft.btitle=Anglicanism%3A+A+Global+Communion&amp;rft.date=1998&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.pages=52&amp;rft.pub=Church+Publishing+Incorporated&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-30"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-30">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Thomas, Charles (1 January 1981). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=BgkQIcRgNk0C&amp;dq=Christianity+arrived+in+Britain&amp;source=gbs_navlinks_s" rel="nofollow"><i>Christianity in Roman Britain to AD 500, Part 500</i></a>. University of California Press. p. 348. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0520043928" title="Special:BookSources/0520043928">0520043928</a>. <q>The horizon we can use to separate sub-Roman and post-Roman Insular Christianity then opens about 475.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAnglicanism&amp;rft.aufirst=Charles&amp;rft.aulast=Thomas&amp;rft.btitle=Christianity+in+Roman+Britain+to+AD+500%2C+Part+500&amp;rft.date=1981-01-01&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DBgkQIcRgNk0C%26dq%3DChristianity%2Barrived%2Bin%2BBritain%26source%3Dgbs_navlinks_s&amp;rft.isbn=0520043928&amp;rft.pages=348&amp;rft.pub=University+of+California+Press&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-31">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Godfrey, C.J. (1962). <i>The Church in Anglo-Saxon England</i>. University of Cambridge Press. <q>Writing after 386, St Ambrose said there were priests in the more isolated countries who countiued to marry; <i>De officiis</i>, 1, 50 (<i>P.L.</i> 16, col. 105).</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAnglicanism&amp;rft.aufirst=C.J.&amp;rft.aulast=Godfrey&amp;rft.btitle=The+Church+in+Anglo-Saxon+England&amp;rft.date=1962&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.pub=University+of+Cambridge+Press&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Boenig-32"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Boenig_32-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Boenig_32-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Boenig, Robert (1 January 2000). <i>Anglo-Saxon Spirituality</i>. Paulist Press. p. 7. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0809105152" title="Special:BookSources/0809105152">0809105152</a>. <q>Not only did the Roman missionaries have to negotiation an accommodation with Germanic and vestigial Celtic paganism, they also had the native Celtic Christianity to deal with. The Celtic Church was largely isolated from the rest of Western Christendom: much of Europe's continent was pagan, and there had been little contact between the Celtic Church and Rome in the years before and during the Anglo-Saxon Migration. As a result the Celtic Church had developed a number of idiosyncrasies that set it apart from the rest of Western Christendom, or at least that is how those adhering to the Roman version of Christianity, like Bede, saw it. Celtic Christianity was dominated by strong abbots and monasteries and characterized by relatively weak bishops (with St. Patrick, a missionary to Ireland from Britain, of course, an exception). This was largely the opposite to the condition that obtained in the Roman Church. The Celtic Church followed, moreover, the eighty-four-year cycle of the dating of Easter rather than the nineteen-year cycle of the Roman Church.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAnglicanism&amp;rft.aufirst=Robert&amp;rft.aulast=Boenig&amp;rft.btitle=Anglo-Saxon+Spirituality&amp;rft.date=2000-01-01&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=0809105152&amp;rft.pages=7&amp;rft.pub=Paulist+Press&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-33"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-33">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book"><i>The Churchman</i>. Oxford University Press. 1881. p. 427. <q>The Roman Church, and those of the Continent, calculated the occurrence of the Easter festival by a new and more accurate method. The Irish and British Churches calculated by an old and defective rule, which they considered had been transmitted to them from St. John. The difference was sometimes so much as a whole month between the Celtic and the Catholic Easter. When the two Churches came into contact, as they did in the North of England, this discrepancy gave rise to scandal and controversy.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAnglicanism&amp;rft.btitle=The+Churchman&amp;rft.date=1881&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.pages=427&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Blair-34"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Blair_34-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Peter Hunter Blair (17 July 2003). <i>An Introduction to Anglo-Saxon England</i>. Cambridge University Press. p. 129. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0521537770" title="Special:BookSources/0521537770">0521537770</a>. <q>During its long period of isolation the Celtic Church had developed in complete independence and had diverged considerably from the paths followed by Rome, not merely in matters of form and ritual, but more fundamentally in its whole organization. Rome could not readily brook the continued existence of what it regarded as schismatic ways and still less could it contemplate that so large a Christian community which showed remarkable missionary zeal should not recognize the pope as its spiritual head.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAnglicanism&amp;rft.au=Peter+Hunter+Blair&amp;rft.btitle=An+Introduction+to+Anglo-Saxon+England&amp;rft.date=2003-07-17&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=0521537770&amp;rft.pages=129&amp;rft.pub=Cambridge+University+Press&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-35"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-35">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Cairns, Earle E. (13 September 2009). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=86cbqi7GpS4C&amp;pg=PT254&amp;dq=Celtic+Christianity+Rome&amp;hl=en&amp;sa=X&amp;ei=DvYYU-nWF8jcyQGkxYDgBw&amp;ved=0CFoQ6AEwBw#v=onepage&amp;q=Celtic%20Christianity%20Rome&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow"><i>Christianity Through the Centuries: A History of the Christian Church</i></a>. Zondervan. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0310829305" title="Special:BookSources/0310829305">0310829305</a>. <q>The Christianity planted in the north by the Celtic Christians soon came into contact with the Roman Catholic Christianity expanding northward from the south of England. The two forms of Christianity differed on many matters. The Celtic Christians did not acknowledge the authority of the pope. They did not always have Easter on the same day of the week each year as the Roman church did. Celtic monks might marry; Roman monks were not permitted to do so. Celtic monks had a different type of tonsure or haircut. These differences caused such confusion and rivalry between the two forms of Christianity that Oswy, who had united most of Anglo-Saxon England under himself, called a meeting at Whitby in 663 to decide which form of Christianity his people would follow.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAnglicanism&amp;rft.aufirst=Earle+E.&amp;rft.aulast=Cairns&amp;rft.btitle=Christianity+Through+the+Centuries%3A+A+History+of+the+Christian+Church&amp;rft.date=2009-09-13&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3D86cbqi7GpS4C%26pg%3DPT254%26dq%3DCeltic%2BChristianity%2BRome%26hl%3Den%26sa%3DX%26ei%3DDvYYU-nWF8jcyQGkxYDgBw%26ved%3D0CFoQ6AEwBw%23v%3Donepage%26q%3DCeltic%2520Christianity%2520Rome%26f%3Dfalse&amp;rft.isbn=0310829305&amp;rft.pub=Zondervan&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-36"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-36">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book"><a href="/wiki/Charles_Chapman_Grafton" title="Charles Chapman Grafton">Charles Chapman Grafton</a> (1911). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=r2IAAAAAMAAJ&amp;printsec=frontcover&amp;dq=charles+grafton+The+Lineage+from+Apostolic+Times+of+the+American+Catholic+Church,+Commonly+Called+the+Episcopal+Church&amp;hl=en&amp;sa=X&amp;ei=8GAZU__dOYTk0QHOkIHQAQ&amp;ved=0CDYQ6AEwAA#v=onepage&amp;q=charles%20grafton%20The%20Lineage%20from%20Apostolic%20Times%20of%20the%20American%20Catholic%20Church%2C%20Commonly%20Called%20the%20Episcopal%20Church&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow"><i>The Lineage from Apostolic Times of the American Catholic Church: Commonly Called the Episcopal Church</i></a>. Young Churchman. p. 69. <q>The Celtic Church, as had been the British, was <i>free from Roman dominion</i>. The case of Wilfrid is one proof of this. Theodore (664) the Archbishop of Canterbury had divided the large See of York. Wilfrid, the Archbishop of York, appealed to Rome. Rome decided in favor of Wilfrid that he should be reinstated in the undivided See. But Archbishope Theodore ignored the papal authority and refused to have Wilfrid reinstated. The great body of the clergy and laity did not stand with Wilfrid. "To all," says Dr. Bright, "the See of Peter was a title of august and sacred import. But they had not as a body, in 678, any notion that gratitude or reverence could bind them to recognize a systematic interference on the part of Rome in their domestic Church matters, by virtue of which any national Church decision might at any time be nullified by a court of appeal sitting beyond the Alps."</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAnglicanism&amp;rft.au=Charles+Chapman+Grafton&amp;rft.btitle=The+Lineage+from+Apostolic+Times+of+the+American+Catholic+Church%3A+Commonly+Called+the+Episcopal+Church&amp;rft.date=1911&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3Dr2IAAAAAMAAJ%26printsec%3Dfrontcover%26dq%3Dcharles%2Bgrafton%2BThe%2BLineage%2Bfrom%2BApostolic%2BTimes%2Bof%2Bthe%2BAmerican%2BCatholic%2BChurch%2C%2BCommonly%2BCalled%2Bthe%2BEpiscopal%2BChurch%26hl%3Den%26sa%3DX%26ei%3D8GAZU__dOYTk0QHOkIHQAQ%26ved%3D0CDYQ6AEwAA%23v%3Donepage%26q%3Dcharles%2520grafton%2520The%2520Lineage%2520from%2520Apostolic%2520Times%2520of%2520the%2520American%2520Catholic%2520Church%252C%2520Commonly%2520Called%2520the%2520Episcopal%2520Church%26f%3Dfalse&amp;rft.pages=69&amp;rft.pub=Young+Churchman&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Blair2-37"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Blair2_37-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Peter Hunter Blair (17 July 2003). <i>An Introduction to Anglo-Saxon England</i>. Cambridge University Press. p. 129. <q>During its long period of isolation the Celtic Church had developed in complete independence and had diverged considerably from the paths followed by Rome, not merely in matters of form and ritual, but more fundamentally in its whole organization. Rome could not readily brook the continued existence of what it regarded as schismatic ways and still less could it contemplate that so large a Christian community which showed remarkable missionary zeal should not recognize the pope as its spiritual head.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAnglicanism&amp;rft.au=Peter+Hunter+Blair&amp;rft.btitle=An+Introduction+to+Anglo-Saxon+England&amp;rft.date=2003-07-17&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.pages=129&amp;rft.pub=Cambridge+University+Press&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-38"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-38">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Taylor, Thomas (1916). <i>The Celtic Christianity of Cornwall</i>. <a href="/wiki/Longman" title="Longman">Longman</a>. <q>Cornwall's independence of Rome implied neither repudiation of nor secession from the Roman Church. It was merely temporary suspension of outward communion with Latin Christianity as a result of political events which had placed Cornwall in a state of isolation.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAnglicanism&amp;rft.aufirst=Thomas&amp;rft.aulast=Taylor&amp;rft.btitle=The+Celtic+Christianity+of+Cornwall&amp;rft.date=1916&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.pub=Longman&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-39"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-39">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">John Robert Wright (2008). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=p5uOeAoHauEC&amp;pg=PA25&amp;dq=Gregorian+Mission+was+Augustine&amp;hl=en&amp;sa=X&amp;ei=EegnU7CZBIilqwGDjoGwCw&amp;ved=0CCkQ6AEwADgK#v=onepage&amp;q=Gregorian%20Mission%20was%20Augustine&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow"><i>A Companion to Bede: A Reader's Commentary on The Ecclesiastical History of the English People</i></a>. Wm. B. Eerdmans Publishing. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0802863094" title="Special:BookSources/0802863094">0802863094</a>. <q>The Gregorian mission under St. Augustine was dispatched from Rome by Pope St. Gregory the Great in 596. It did not arrive in England until 597. The story of this mission has much in common with the evangelical fervor displayed by Bede in earlier chapters. It was in fact the first papal mission to a pagan people.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAnglicanism&amp;rft.au=John+Robert+Wright&amp;rft.btitle=A+Companion+to+Bede%3A+A+Reader%27s+Commentary+on+The+Ecclesiastical+History+of+the+English+People&amp;rft.date=2008&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3Dp5uOeAoHauEC%26pg%3DPA25%26dq%3DGregorian%2BMission%2Bwas%2BAugustine%26hl%3Den%26sa%3DX%26ei%3DEegnU7CZBIilqwGDjoGwCw%26ved%3D0CCkQ6AEwADgK%23v%3Donepage%26q%3DGregorian%2520Mission%2520was%2520Augustine%26f%3Dfalse&amp;rft.isbn=0802863094&amp;rft.pub=Wm.+B.+Eerdmans+Publishing&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-40"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-40">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Louis Wilken (27 November 2012). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=iW1-JImrwQUC&amp;pg=PA275&amp;dq=Augustine+evangelize+Kent&amp;hl=en&amp;sa=X&amp;ei=4gkoU7_kD4qpyAHJj4D4DQ&amp;ved=0CFgQ6AEwBg#v=onepage&amp;q=Augustine%20evangelize%20Kent&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow"><i>The First Thousand Years: A Global History of Christianity Robert</i></a>. Yale University Press. pp. 274–275. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0300118848" title="Special:BookSources/0300118848">0300118848</a>. <q>When Augustine arrive there were some Christians living in Kent, and a church building near the city from Roman times was still standing. The king put it at the disposal of Augustine's monks. The presence of Christians in Kent suggests that Gregory's aim in sending Augustine was not only to evangelize the native population but also to bring ecclesiastical order to the region and establish "Roman" Christianity in the British Isles.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAnglicanism&amp;rft.au=Louis+Wilken&amp;rft.btitle=The+First+Thousand+Years%3A+A+Global+History+of+Christianity+Robert&amp;rft.date=2012-11-27&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DiW1-JImrwQUC%26pg%3DPA275%26dq%3DAugustine%2Bevangelize%2BKent%26hl%3Den%26sa%3DX%26ei%3D4gkoU7_kD4qpyAHJj4D4DQ%26ved%3D0CFgQ6AEwBg%23v%3Donepage%26q%3DAugustine%2520evangelize%2520Kent%26f%3Dfalse&amp;rft.isbn=0300118848&amp;rft.pages=274-275&amp;rft.pub=Yale+University+Press&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-41"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-41">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Wallace, Martin. <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=pGLstPf5iJMC&amp;pg=PA9&amp;dq=Towards+the+end+of+the+sixth+century+a+great+spiritual+leader,+Gregory,+became+Pope.+He+inherited+a+Church+which+was+very+weak+and+disorganized.+Consequently+he+set+about+sending+key+people+throughout+the+continent+to+organize+this+Church,+and+bring+some+sense+of+cohesion.+To+these+islands+he+sent+St+Augustine...to+bring+the+wayward+Celtic+churches+into+line.+Then,+as+now,+the+life+of+those+in+the+British+Isles+tended+to+be+rather+separate+from+the+rest+of+the+continent.&amp;hl=en&amp;sa=X&amp;ei=heonU-rgK-rJygHrv4CYAw&amp;ved=0CCwQ6AEwAA#v=onepage&amp;q=Towards%20the%20end%20of%20the%20sixth%20century%20a%20great%20spiritual%20leader%2C%20Gregory%2C%20became%20Pope.%20He%20inherited%20a%20Church%20which%20was%20very%20weak%20and%20disorganized.%20Consequently%20he%20set%20about%20sending%20key%20people%20throughout%20the%20continent%20to%20organize%20this%20Church%2C%20and%20bring%20some%20sense%20of%20cohesion.%20To%20these%20islands%20he%20sent%20St%20Augustine...to%20bring%20the%20wayward%20Celtic%20churches%20into%20line.%20Then%2C%20as%20now%2C%20the%20life%20of%20those%20in%20the%20British%20Isles%20tended%20to%20be%20rather%20separate%20from%20the%20rest%20of%20the%20continent.&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow"><i>Celtic Resource Book</i></a>. Church House Publishing. p. 9. <q>Towards the end of the sixth century a great spiritual leader, Gregory, became Pope. He inherited a Church which was very weak and disorganized. Consequently he set about sending key people throughout the continent to organize this Church, and bring some sense of cohesion. To these islands he sent St Augustine...to bring the wayward Celtic churches into line. Then, as now, the life of those in the British Isles tended to be rather separate from the rest of the continent.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAnglicanism&amp;rft.aufirst=Martin&amp;rft.aulast=Wallace&amp;rft.btitle=Celtic+Resource+Book&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DpGLstPf5iJMC%26pg%3DPA9%26dq%3DTowards%2Bthe%2Bend%2Bof%2Bthe%2Bsixth%2Bcentury%2Ba%2Bgreat%2Bspiritual%2Bleader%2C%2BGregory%2C%2Bbecame%2BPope.%2BHe%2Binherited%2Ba%2BChurch%2Bwhich%2Bwas%2Bvery%2Bweak%2Band%2Bdisorganized.%2BConsequently%2Bhe%2Bset%2Babout%2Bsending%2Bkey%2Bpeople%2Bthroughout%2Bthe%2Bcontinent%2Bto%2Borganize%2Bthis%2BChurch%2C%2Band%2Bbring%2Bsome%2Bsense%2Bof%2Bcohesion.%2BTo%2Bthese%2Bislands%2Bhe%2Bsent%2BSt%2BAugustine...to%2Bbring%2Bthe%2Bwayward%2BCeltic%2Bchurches%2Binto%2Bline.%2BThen%2C%2Bas%2Bnow%2C%2Bthe%2Blife%2Bof%2Bthose%2Bin%2Bthe%2BBritish%2BIsles%2Btended%2Bto%2Bbe%2Brather%2Bseparate%2Bfrom%2Bthe%2Brest%2Bof%2Bthe%2Bcontinent.%26hl%3Den%26sa%3DX%26ei%3DheonU-rgK-rJygHrv4CYAw%26ved%3D0CCwQ6AEwAA%23v%3Donepage%26q%3DTowards%2520the%2520end%2520of%2520the%2520sixth%2520century%2520a%2520great%2520spiritual%2520leader%252C%2520Gregory%252C%2520became%2520Pope.%2520He%2520inherited%2520a%2520Church%2520which%2520was%2520very%2520weak%2520and%2520disorganized.%2520Consequently%2520he%2520set%2520about%2520sending%2520key%2520people%2520throughout%2520the%2520continent%2520to%2520organize%2520this%2520Church%252C%2520and%2520bring%2520some%2520sense%2520of%2520cohesion.%2520To%2520these%2520islands%2520he%2520sent%2520St%2520Augustine...to%2520bring%2520the%2520wayward%2520Celtic%2520churches%2520into%2520line.%2520Then%252C%2520as%2520now%252C%2520the%2520life%2520of%2520those%2520in%2520the%2520British%2520Isles%2520tended%2520to%2520be%2520rather%2520separate%2520from%2520the%2520rest%2520of%2520the%2520continent.%26f%3Dfalse&amp;rft.pages=9&amp;rft.pub=Church+House+Publishing&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span <li id="cite_note-46"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-46">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Christopher L. <li id="cite_note-47"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-47">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://anglican.org/church/ChurchHistory.html" rel="nofollow">"Church History"</a>. <i>The Anglican Domain</i>. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">18 March</span> 2014</span>. <q>The Anglican Church evolved as part of the Roman church, but the Celtic influence was folded back into the Roman portion of the church in many ways, perhaps most notably by Charlemagne's tutor Aidan.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAnglicanism&amp;rft.atitle=Church+History&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fanglican.org%2Fchurch%2FChurchHistory.html&amp;rft.jtitle=The+Anglican+Domain&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-48"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-48">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://www.oikoumene.org/en/church-families/anglican-churches" rel="nofollow">"Anglican churches"</a>. World Council of Churches<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">18 March</span> 2014</span>. <q>Deriving from the ancient Celtic and Saxon churches of the British Isles, Anglicanism found its distinctive identity in the 16th- and 17th-century Reformation, when the separate Church of England, Church of Ireland and Scottish Episcopal Church came into being.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAnglicanism&amp;rft.btitle=Anglican+churches&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.oikoumene.org%2Fen%2Fchurch-families%2Fanglican-churches&amp;rft.pub=World+Council+of+Churches&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>

<li id="cite_note-54"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-54">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Vicki K. Black (1 August 2005). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=u6DU_WJEdGYC&amp;pg=PA11&amp;dq=book+of+common+prayer+reformation&amp;hl=en&amp;sa=X&amp;ei=QwYoU-aNIYaTyQHr1YHgBg&amp;ved=0CGEQ6AEwBw#v=onepage&amp;q=book%20of%20common%20prayer%20reformation&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow"><i>Welcome to the Book of Common Prayer</i></a>. Church Publishing Incorporated. p. 129. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0819226017" title="Special:BookSources/0819226017">0819226017</a>. <q>With the revision of the English Book of Common Prayer in 1662 under King Charles II, the church in England solidified its moderate position between the Puritans on the one hand and the more Catholic high church party on the other. Charles restored prayer book worship to England after the decade of Presbyterianism under Oliver Cromwell, and his 1662 Book of Common Prayer has provided the basis for subsequent revisions throughout the Anglican Communion. In England itself, it is still the official prayer book, despite repeated efforts to approve a revision.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAnglicanism&amp;rft.au=Vicki+K.+Black&amp;rft.btitle=Welcome+to+the+Book+of+Common+Prayer&amp;rft.date=2005-08-01&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3Du6DU_WJEdGYC%26pg%3DPA11%26dq%3Dbook%2Bof%2Bcommon%2Bprayer%2Breformation%26hl%3Den%26sa%3DX%26ei%3DQwYoU-aNIYaTyQHr1YHgBg%26ved%3D0CGEQ6AEwBw%23v%3Donepage%26q%3Dbook%2520of%2520common%2520prayer%2520reformation%26f%3Dfalse&amp;rft.isbn=0819226017&amp;rft.pages=129&amp;rft.pub=Church+Publishing+Incorporated&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>

Black (1 August 2005). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=u6DU_WJEdGYC&amp;pg=PA11&amp;dq=book+of+common+prayer+reformation&amp;hl=en&amp;sa=X&amp;ei=QwYoU-aNIYaTyQHr1YHgBg&amp;ved=0CGEQ6AEwBw#v=onepage&amp;q=book%20of%20common%20prayer%20reformation&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow"><i>Welcome to the Book of Common Prayer</i></a>. <li id="cite_note-79"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-79">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Nockles, P. B. (1994). <i>The Oxford Movement in Context – Anglican High Churchmanship, 1760–1857</i>. Cambridge University Press. pp. 7–8, 113, 125, 127. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-521-38162-2" title="Special:BookSources/0-521-38162-2">0-521-38162-2</a>. <a href="/wiki/OCLC" title="OCLC">OCLC</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/oclc/185319963" rel="nofollow">185319963</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAnglicanism&amp;rft.aufirst=P.+B.&amp;rft.aulast=Nockles&amp;rft.btitle=The+Oxford+Movement+in+Context+%93+Anglican+High+Churchmanship%2C+1760%931857&amp;rft.date=1994&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=info%3Aoclcnum%2F185319963&amp;rft.isbn=0-521-38162-2&amp;rft.pages=7-8%2C+113%2C+125%2C+127&amp;rft.pub=Cambridge+University+Press&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-80"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-80">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="http://justus.anglican.org/resources/bio/249.html" rel="nofollow">Accessed 9 November 2010.</a></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-81"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-81">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The 19th century Evangelical interpretation of the Prayerbook, now less frequent, included celebration of Holy Communion while the priest was standing at the northern <i>short side</i> of the communion table. This misinterpretation was caused by the fact that the 1662 <i>Book of Common Prayer</i> retained two contradictory rubrics. From 1552 a rubric was retained that the priest should stand at the northern <i>long side</i> of a communion table standing east-west in the choir (the communicants sitting in the choir stalls by the northern and southern walls). From 1559 was retained the rubric that 'the chancels shall remain as they have done in times past', originally intended to protect the mediaeval interior of church buildings from calvinist vandalism, and – mainly neglected during the reigns of Elizabeth I and James I – it was not consented to generally before the reign of Charles II. During the reign of Elizabeth I, only the <a href="/wiki/Chapel_Royal" title="Chapel Royal">Chapels Royal</a> retained the mediaeval position of the communion table, standing permanently north-south at the east wall of the choir. The parish of St. Giles Cripplegate, London, began to apply the Chapels Royal arrangement of the communion table in 1599 or 1605, and from there it began to spread. Archbishop Laud's attempt to make it mandatory in the 1630s backfired, with well known consequences. By the reign of Charles II, however, it was applied generally, and the original intention of the <i>northward position rubric</i> became unintelligible, and easily misunderstood.</span></li>
